Source: 2026-05-31 ACID on object storage: how Delta Lake’s transaction log actually works | by Victor José | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: ACID on object storage: how Delta Lake’s transaction log actually works | by Victor José | Medium url: https://medium.com/@victorjmp9/acid-on-object-storage-how-delta-lakes-transaction-log-actually-works-7b14db804234 date: 2026-05-31T00:46:57.599Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Delta Lake’s symbol, downloaded from here Object storage systems like S3, Azure Blob Storage, and Google Cloud Storage are eventually consistent, append-only systems with no built-in coordination mechanisms. They’re designed for durability and scale, not for database-style operations. Yet Delta Lake manages to provide full ACID guarantees on top of these systems. Almost as if it abuses its simplicity to build something complex. The secret lies in the transaction log, a simple approach that uses JSON files as a coordination layer. Understanding how this works reveals why Delta Lake can provide database guarantees without requiring a traditional database infrastructure. ACID properties: what they actually mean Before getting into the specifics on how Delta Lake achieves ACID properties, we need first to discuss what are them. This is a specific set of guarantees that determine whether your data operations are reliable: Atomicity: either all parts of a transaction complete successfully, or none of them do. If you’re inserting 1000 records and the process crashes after 500, you get zero records, not 500. No partial states exist. Consistency: data always moves from one valid state to another valid state. Schema rules, constraints, and business logic are enforced. You can’t accidentally insert a string into an integer column or violate uniqueness constraints. Isolation: concurrent transactions don’t interfere with each other. When two operations happen simultaneously, the result is the same as if they happened sequentially in some order. Readers don’t see partially completed writes. Durability: once a transaction commits, it survives system crashes, power failures, and other disasters. The changes are permanent and recoverable. Traditional databases achieve these properties through sophisticated mechanisms: write-ahead logs, lock managers, buffer pools, and crash recovery algorithms. Object storage provides none of these building blocks, making ACID properties seem impossible to achieve. This is exactly what makes Delta Lake’s approach remarkable, because it provides full database-level ACID guarantees using only the basic primitives that object storage systems offer. The object storage challenge Traditional databases achieve ACID properties through WAL (Write-Ahead Logging), locks, and in-place updates. Object storage provides none of these primitives: No atomic operations across files: you can’t atomically create multiple files or ensure two operations happen together. No file locking: multiple writers can’t coordinate access to prevent conflicts. No in-place updates: files are immutable once written. “Updates” mean writing entirely new files. Eventual consistency: recent writes might not be immediately visible to all readers. Consider what happens when you try to update a Parquet-based data lake: There’s no coordination mechanism to prevent this race condition or ensure both changes are preserved. The transaction log architecture Delta Lake solves this through a transaction log stored as a sequence of JSON files in the _delta_log/ directory: Each JSON file represents one atomic transaction. The filename encodes the version number with zero-padding, creating a natural ordering that’s consistent across distributed systems. Transaction log structure Each transaction log file contains an array of actions describing what changed: The great catch here: each JSON file is written atomically. Object storage guarantees that file writes are atomic, the file either exists completely or not at all. Delta Lake leverages this single guarantee to build full ACID properties. Brilliant, innit? Literally using artifices that already exist to give object storage ACID properties. Optimistic concurrency control When multiple writers attempt to modify the same table, Delta Lake uses optimistic concurrency control: Read phase: each writer reads the current version number and table state Write phase: each writer stages their changes and prepares a transaction log entry Commit phase: each writer attempts to write the next sequential transaction log file Only one writer can successfully create 00000000000000000005.json. Others receive an error and must retry by: Reading the new current state Checking if their changes conflict with what was committed Either applying their changes to the new state or failing the transaction This approach converts the eventual consistency problem into a conflict detection mechanism. Instead of requiring coordination before writing, Delta Lake detects conflicts after attempting to write and handles them through retries. ACID properties implementation Atomicity through single file writes Each transaction is represented by exactly one JSON file. Since object storage guarantees atomic file writes, either the entire transaction commits (file exists) or nothing commits (file doesn’t exist). Consider a complex transaction that adds three new Parquet files: Even though three Parquet files were written separately, the transaction only commits when the JSON file is successfully written. If the process crashes after writing two Parquet files but before writing the JSON file, those orphaned Parquet files are ignored, meaning that they’re not part of any committed transaction. Consistency through schema evolution controls The metaData action in each transaction includes the complete schema: Schema changes require explicit metadata actions. Delta Lake validates that: New columns are nullable or have defaults Column type changes are safe (widening only) Required columns aren’t dropped This prevents the schema drift that breaks downstream consumers in traditional data lakes. Isolation through snapshot reads Each transaction log file represents an immutable snapshot. When you start reading a Delta table, you read the current version number and work exclusively with that snapshot: Writers don’t interfere with readers because they create new versions rather than modifying existing data. The immutable nature of object storage becomes an advantage for isolation. Durability through persistent transaction log Once a transaction log file is written to object storage, it’s durable. The distributed nature of object storage (multiple replicas, geographic distribution) provides higher durability guarantees than most traditional databases. Delta Lake doesn’t require additional backup mechanisms for the transaction log itself because the object storage system handles durability. Performance implications Transaction log read performance Reading table metadata requires scanning the transaction log. For frequently accessed tables, this creates a performance bottleneck. Delta Lake optimizes this through: Caching: recent transaction log entries are cached in memory Checkpointing: reduces the number of files to read for table state Statistics: file-level statistics enable data skipping without reading data files Write amplification Every change requires writing a new transaction log file, even for single-row updates. This creates write amplification compared to traditional databases with in-place updates. However, the trade-off enables: Lock-free concurrent reads during writes Perfect snapshot isolation Automatic versioning and time travel capabilities Optimistic concurrency overhead High-conflict workloads (many writers modifying the same partitions) result in transaction retry storms. In practice, most analytical workloads have natural write isolation (different time partitions, different data sources) that minimizes conflicts. Why this design works Delta Lake’s transaction log succeeds because it aligns with object storage strengths rather than fighting against object storage limitations: Leverages atomic file writes: the only guarantee object storage provides becomes the foundation for full ACID properties Embraces immutability: instead of requiring in-place updates, the design makes immutability an advantage for isolation and consistency Uses eventual consistency positively: readers can work with slightly stale versions without correctness issues Scales horizontally: more writers create more transaction log files, but don’t create lock contention The elegance lies in its simplicity: using JSON files and sequential numbering to coordinate distributed systems. No external coordination service, no complex consensus protocols, no shared state beyond the object storage itself. This architecture explains why Delta Lake can provide database-level guarantees while running on commodity object storage, and why it scales to thousands of concurrent readers and writers without traditional database bottlenecks. Also, since the actual data read in the Delta Lake is open format (parquet), you’re not locked in any specific vendor to reap its benefits of better governance than a Data Lake.

Source: 2026-05-31 How Apache Iceberg Actually Works | by Thomas F McGeehan V | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: How Apache Iceberg Actually Works | by Thomas F McGeehan V | Medium url: https://medium.com/@tfmv/how-apache-iceberg-actually-works-64f97fb13c45 date: 2026-05-31T00:46:43.598Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Apache Iceberg has emerged as the definitive winner in the table format wars of 2024–2025. With Databricks acquiring Tabular (founded by Iceberg’s creators), Snowflake launching Polaris (an Iceberg-based catalog), and major cloud providers standardizing on Iceberg, it’s clear that this isn’t just another data technology. Apache Iceberg isn’t just a table format; it’s the foundation of the modern data lakehouse, turning chaos into a structured, reliable, and blazing-fast platform. But what makes Iceberg so revolutionary? And more importantly, how does it actually work under the hood? This guide will take you on a journey from the fundamental problems Iceberg solves to the cutting-edge features arriving in 2025. The Data Lake Problems Iceberg Was Built to Solve Every data engineer who has wrangled a large data lake knows the pain points. Traditional Hive-style tables on HDFS or cloud storage tend to accumulate a lot of baggage over time. Apache Iceberg was created to tackle several of these very real problems head-on: The Small Files Problem As data volumes grow, you often end up with thousands of tiny files scattered across your data lake. Each file requires separate I/O operations, metadata tracking, and planning overhead. A simple query on a day’s data can take minutes just to plan because it has to scan every partition directory and open thousands of files. Data Corruption and Inconsistency Without proper ACID guarantees, concurrent writes or job failures leave behind partial data. Maybe one process deletes rows by overwriting files while another reads mid-change; the result is often half-updated data and corruption. Traditional “append-only” lakes had no notion of atomic commit, so readers could see a table in a mixed state. Brittle Partitioning Schemes In older systems, how you partitioned the data was something every user had to know. If the table was partitioned by date, but a user’s query forgot to filter on that column, it would inadvertently scan the whole dataset. The Hive format exposed physical partition details to the user; changing the partitioning later meant reorganizing tons of files. Painful Schema Changes Evolving a table’s schema (adding columns, changing data types) used to be a risky operation. In Hive you might have to rebuild the table or live with awkward hacks. Many teams avoided schema updates altogether because renaming or removing a column could break downstream jobs or require rewriting all your data. Apache Iceberg squarely addresses these issues with a sophisticated metadata-driven approach that brings reliability and manageability to data lakes. What Exactly Is Apache Iceberg? Iceberg is a table format for large analytic datasets; essentially a set of rules and metadata that define how to treat a bunch of data files as a coherent table. It’s not a processing engine or a file format by itself. Your data files remain in familiar formats like Parquet, ORC, or Avro. Iceberg is the software layer that tracks those files and presents them as a single table, with SQL semantics, to any execution engine. To clarify, Apache Iceberg is not a query engine or a storage system: it doesn’t replace Spark or Trino, and it doesn’t store bytes in a new format. Instead, it’s a specification (and library implementations) for how to manage table metadata. This means you can use Iceberg tables with your engine of choice; Spark, Flink, Trino, Presto, Hive, Impala can all read and write the same Iceberg table in a transactionally consistent way. The Architecture: How Iceberg Tables Are Structured Understanding Iceberg’s architecture is key to appreciating its power. Let’s break down the layered structure that makes everything possible: Catalog Layer: The Entry Point This is the entry point that maps a table name to its current metadata file. The catalog can be a Hive Metastore, AWS Glue, a relational DB, or even just a special folder. It stores a pointer like “for db1.table1, the latest table metadata is at s3://.../metadata/00042.metadata.json ". Metadata File: The Master Record This is a JSON (or Avro) file that describes the table’s state and structure at a high level. It includes: The table schema with column definitions and types Partitioning specifications (which can evolve over time) A list of snapshots with their IDs, timestamps, and manifest list pointers Sort orders and table properties Manifest List: The Table of Contents A manifest list is a small file that lists all the manifest files that make up a given snapshot. If a snapshot has many data files, Iceberg groups them into multiple manifests for manageability. The manifest list includes summary statistics for each manifest. Manifest Files: The Detailed Index Manifests are where the rubber meets the road. A manifest file is an index of actual data files. Each manifest lists a subset of the table’s data files with rich metadata: File path and size Row count and column statistics (min/max values, null counts) Partition values File format and compression details Data Files: The Actual Data Finally, at the bottom, are the actual Parquet/ORC/Avro files holding your table’s data. The difference is that Iceberg knows exactly which data files belong to the table and to each snapshot through its manifest system. Inside Iceberg: Snapshots, Transactions, and Time Travel The true power of Iceberg lies in how it manages table state over time. Instead of treating a table as a mutable set of files in a directory, Iceberg treats it as an ever-evolving series of snapshots. Snapshots: Immutable Table Versions When you insert, delete, or update data in an Iceberg table, it doesn’t brute-force overwrite everything. Instead, it creates a new snapshot of the table. Each snapshot is like a commit in a version control system, representing a consistent view of the entire table at a point in time. Crucially, the old snapshot is still preserved (until you clean it up). This is how Iceberg provides time travel and isolated reads: a reader will either see the old snapshot or the new snapshot, but nothing in between. The switch happens atomically by updating the metadata pointer to the new snapshot file. ACID Transactions and Concurrent Writes By using snapshots and atomic pointer swaps, Iceberg achieves ACID transactions on the data lake. With Iceberg, the data lake sheds its wild roots; ACID guarantees and snapshot isolation bring database-level trust to object storage, no compromises needed. The transaction model is optimistic concurrency control with snapshot isolation: Each writer assumes it can make its changes Writers prepare new manifests/metadata based on a specific snapshot Writers attempt to commit by swapping in their new snapshot If another writer committed first, the swap fails and the writer retries This approach ensures that multiple jobs can write to the table simultaneously without corrupting it, while readers always get a consistent view. Time Travel: Querying the Past One of Iceberg’s most powerful features is time travel; the ability to query a table at an earlier point in time. This works because all snapshots are tracked in the table’s metadata log. Advanced Features Hidden Partitioning: Smarter Data Organization Traditional partitioning exposed physical layout details to users. Iceberg’s hidden partitioning and schema evolution mean your data lake bends without breaking; ready for whatever analytics throw at it, today or tomorrow. Iceberg’s hidden partitioning abstracts this away: Iceberg automatically translates the timestamp filter into the appropriate partition pruning, even though the user never mentioned the partition column. Schema Evolution: Change Without Pain Iceberg identifies columns by stable IDs under the hood, not just by position. This enables safe schema evolution: Old files continue to work because Iceberg maps column IDs correctly, treating missing fields as null and ignoring dropped columns. Row-Level Operations: Updates and Deletes Iceberg v2 introduced sophisticated support for row-level operations through delete files: Position Deletes: Track specific row positions to delete Equality Deletes: Track rows to delete based on column values (ideal for CDC) Format Version 3: The Future is Here Apache Iceberg v3, arriving in 2025, brings revolutionary new capabilities: New Data Types for Modern Workloads Row Lineage and Advanced CDC Row lineage enables efficient change data capture by tracking row evolution: Materialized Views V3 introduces native materialized view support: Performance Optimization: Making Iceberg Sing File Layout Strategies Compaction is crucial for maintaining performance: Z-Ordering for Multi-Dimensional Queries Z-ordering optimizes data layout for queries across multiple dimensions: Partition Evolution Iceberg allows you to change partitioning schemes without rewriting data: Real-World Use Cases: Iceberg in Action Before diving into the numbers, let’s see how organizations are leveraging Iceberg’s capabilities in production: Real-Time Analytics at Scale A major media company processes 50TB of streaming click data daily using Iceberg with Apache Flink. By leveraging merge-on-read for high-frequency updates and hidden partitioning by hour(timestamp), they achieve sub-second query response times for real-time dashboards while maintaining full historical data for trend analysis. The same table serves both streaming ingestion and batch analytics without any data duplication. Reproducible Machine Learning Workflows Data scientists at a leading tech firm rely on Iceberg’s time travel capabilities to version their training datasets. When a model’s performance degrades, they can instantly roll back to the exact snapshot used for the previous training run, ensuring reproducible experiments. Schema evolution allows them to add new features without breaking existing ML pipelines, while ACID guarantees prevent corrupted training data from partial ETL failures. Cross-Engine Data Mesh Architecture An enterprise retail company runs a federated data mesh where different teams use their preferred tools on shared Iceberg tables. Marketing runs ad-hoc queries with Trino for campaign analysis; data engineering processes batch jobs with Spark for ETL; and the real-time team uses Flink for streaming aggregations, all reading and writing the same tables with full consistency. No data silos, no duplication, no conflicts. Change Data Capture for Financial Services A financial services firm uses Iceberg’s equality delete files to implement efficient CDC from their transactional systems. Updates to customer records are captured as delete files marking old versions obsolete, while new versions are appended. This approach provides a complete audit trail for regulatory compliance while enabling fast incremental processing; queries only scan changed data, not the entire dataset. Real-World Performance: The Numbers Don’t Lie Recent benchmarks show Iceberg’s performance advantages: Query Planning Speed Traditional Hive: Minutes to plan queries on large tables Iceberg: Sub-second planning even on petabyte tables File Skipping Efficiency Without optimization: Scan 5.4M rows With Z-ordering: Scan 1.2M rows (2.5x improvement) Query time: 5.0s → 2.1s (2.4x faster) Storage Efficiency Upsolver-optimized Iceberg: 77GB table, 78GB storage AWS Glue compaction: 144GB table, 1,600GB storage (20x overhead) File count: 141 vs 326 files (2.3x fewer files) The Ecosystem: Iceberg in 2025 Catalogs and Services Best Practices: Lessons from Production Table Design Partition wisely: Aim for 100MB-1GB partitions Choose sort orders: Based on common query patterns Monitor file sizes: Keep data files 100MB-1GB Plan for growth: Design schemas for evolution Maintenance Operations Performance Monitoring Common Pitfalls and How to Avoid Them The Small Files Problem Problem: Streaming ingestion creates thousands of tiny files Solution: Implement continuous compaction with appropriate triggers Metadata Bloat Problem: Too many snapshots slow down planning Solution: Regular snapshot expiration based on retention needs Partition Proliferation Problem: Over-partitioning creates too many small partitions Solution: Choose partition granularity based on query patterns and data volume Write Amplification Problem: Copy-on-write updates rewrite entire files Solution: Use merge-on-read for high-update workloads; compact periodically Migration Strategy: Getting to Iceberg Successfully Organizations looking to migrate to Apache Iceberg should focus on the migration process itself as the primary challenge. While Iceberg’s benefits are compelling, the journey from legacy systems requires careful planning and the right tools. Focus on Migration First My personal recommendation is to prioritize the migration strategy over feature exploration. Too many organizations get caught up in Iceberg’s advanced capabilities before establishing a solid migration foundation. The most successful migrations follow this approach: Start with a pilot: Choose 2–3 non-critical tables for initial migration Establish patterns: Develop repeatable migration processes Scale gradually: Expand to more critical workloads once patterns are proven Optimize later: Focus on performance tuning after migration is complete Recommended Migration Tool: OLake by Datazip For organizations serious about Iceberg migration, I strongly recommend OLake by Datazip. This open-source platform provides efficient, scalable data ingestion specifically designed for lakehouse formats. Why OLake stands out: 5x faster replication: Optimized specifically for lakehouse architectures Real-time capabilities: Supports both streaming and batch ingestion Iceberg-native: Built from the ground up for Iceberg table format Open source: No vendor lock-in, full transparency Production-ready: Used by organizations processing 50TB+ daily Key OLake capabilities: Migration Best Practices Assessment Phase: Inventory existing tables and their access patterns Identify dependencies and downstream consumers Estimate data volumes and growth rates Plan for schema evolution needs Execution Phase: Use tools like OLake for automated, reliable migration Implement parallel processing for large datasets Maintain data lineage throughout the process Test thoroughly before switching production workloads Validation Phase: Compare row counts and checksums between source and target Validate query performance meets requirements Ensure all downstream applications work correctly Document any schema or query changes needed Alternative Migration Tools While OLake is my top recommendation, other tools worth considering include: Iceberg Catalog Migrator: For catalog-to-catalog migrations without data movement Netflix’s Hive2Iceberg: Reference implementation for Hive migrations Spark Migration Procedures: Built-in Spark procedures for table conversion Custom ETL: Using Spark or Flink for complex migration scenarios The key is choosing a tool that matches your specific migration complexity and organizational capabilities. Looking Ahead: Apache Iceberg in 2026 and Beyond As we look toward 2026 and beyond, Apache Iceberg’s evolution will be shaped by transformative trends in infrastructure, AI, and data processing. Here’s what I believe the Apache community should focus on to maintain Iceberg’s leadership position. AI-Native Data Infrastructure Intelligent Table Optimization: The future of Iceberg lies in AI-driven automation. We should develop machine learning models that automatically optimize table layouts, partition strategies, and compaction schedules based on query patterns and data characteristics. Vector Storage Integration: As AI workloads become dominant, Iceberg needs native support for vector embeddings and similarity search. This includes: Vector data types in the Iceberg specification Optimized storage formats for high-dimensional data Integration with vector databases for hybrid OLAP/vector workloads Zero-Code ETL Revolution Declarative Data Pipelines: The future belongs to declarative, intent-based data management. Iceberg should support: Natural Language Interfaces: Integration with large language models for natural language data operations: “Create a table for customer events with automatic partitioning” “Optimize the sales table for time-series queries” “Set up CDC from our PostgreSQL orders table” Infrastructure Evolution Serverless-First Architecture: Iceberg should be optimized for serverless computing environments: Instant cold starts for query engines Pay-per-query pricing models Automatic scaling based on workload demands Edge computing support for global data access Quantum-Ready Cryptography: As quantum computing advances, Iceberg needs quantum-resistant encryption: Post-quantum cryptographic algorithms Quantum key distribution for ultra-secure data Homomorphic encryption for privacy-preserving analytics Advanced Catalog Capabilities Multi-Dimensional Versioning: Beyond time travel, Iceberg should support: Feature versioning for ML model training Environment-specific data views (dev/staging/prod) Semantic versioning for schema changes Cross-table transaction boundaries Federated Global Catalogs: Support for planet-scale data management: Cross-region catalog synchronization Geo-distributed metadata with eventual consistency Regulatory compliance automation (GDPR, CCPA) Data sovereignty enforcement Performance Breakthroughs Hardware Acceleration: Native support for emerging hardware: GPU-accelerated query processing FPGA-optimized file formats Persistent memory integration Optical storage for archival data Adaptive Query Execution: Real-time query optimization based on: Historical query patterns Current system load Data distribution changes Cost optimization across cloud providers Ecosystem Integration Universal Interoperability: Iceberg as the universal data exchange format: Native support in all major cloud services Real-time synchronization with operational databases Seamless integration with streaming platforms Cross-cloud data sharing without movement Sustainability Focus: Environmental responsibility in data management: Carbon footprint tracking for queries Automatic data lifecycle management Energy-efficient storage formats Green computing optimizations Community and Governance Decentralized Development: DAO-style governance for major decisions Incentive mechanisms for contributors Global developer education programs Industry-academia collaboration initiatives Standards Leadership: Position Iceberg as the foundation for: Open data sharing protocols Cross-industry data standards Regulatory compliance frameworks Ethical AI data practices 2026 Priority Roadmap Immediate Focus (2026): AI-native optimization engine Vector storage capabilities Serverless performance improvements Enhanced security and privacy features Medium-term Goals (2027–2028): Natural language interfaces Quantum-ready cryptography Global federated catalogs Hardware acceleration support Long-term Vision (2029+): Fully autonomous data management Universal data interoperability Sustainable computing integration Decentralized governance model The future of Apache Iceberg isn’t just about better table formats; it’s about creating the foundational layer for humanity’s data infrastructure. By focusing on these areas, we can ensure Iceberg remains the cornerstone of data architecture for decades to come. Why Iceberg Won Apache Iceberg succeeded because it solved real problems with elegant engineering: Metadata-driven architecture enables fast query planning Snapshot isolation provides ACID guarantees without locks Hidden partitioning abstracts physical layout complexity Schema evolution makes changes safe and seamless Open ecosystem prevents vendor lock-in As we move into 2025, the transformation is complete. What started as a solution to the small files problem has become something much bigger. Apache Iceberg isn’t just a table format; it’s the foundation of the modern data lakehouse, turning chaos into a structured, reliable, and blazing-fast platform. Whether you’re building real-time analytics, implementing data mesh architectures, or scaling to petabytes, understanding how Iceberg works gives you the knowledge to build systems that are both powerful and reliable. From the streaming analytics powering real-time dashboards to the ML workflows ensuring reproducible science, from the cross-engine data mesh eliminating silos to the CDC systems maintaining perfect audit trails; Iceberg has proven that the data lake doesn’t have to be wild to be powerful. The data lake is dead. Long live the lakehouse. And at its heart beats Apache Iceberg. This guide represents the state of Apache Iceberg as of early 2025. The project continues to evolve rapidly, with new features and optimizations being added regularly. For the latest updates, consult the official Apache Iceberg documentation. Code by instinct. Source: 2026-05-31 Delta Lake Open Source Project. The first unified data management… | by Eric Bellet | Medium

WebSync metadata title: Delta Lake Open Source Project. The first unified data management… | by Eric Bellet | Medium url: https://medium.com/@belleteric93/delta-lake-open-source-project-3146463b4998 date: 2026-05-31T00:46:30.654Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap The first unified data management system. Delta Lake is an open source project that brings a lot of important features to Apache Spark and big data workloads. The Evolution of Big data The current state of platforms Delta Lake Delta Lake Under the Hood Delta Architecture The Evolution of Big data All started in the 70s when the companies had all this data in their databases  about how their products were doing and different revenue in different region and so on, but they couldn’t actually put it all together they were flying blind and people came up with this concept of a data warehouse and the companies decided to take the most important data that they have in their different databases, and let’s ETL  (extract, transform, and load) them all into a central data warehouse  and they could get business intelligence, so the data was in it was really pristine it was reliable it was cleaned up with a particular scheme, and they could do really fast queries on it so these were really performant systems because they had such control over the data that they’d put in there you could actually optimize it and get really great performance out of it. Finally, it gave you transactional reliability which meant that you could have lots of different business analysts using these Business Intelligence tools simultaneously and using concurrently the same data warehouse and it just worked, no errors, no corruption so this was a great thing about data warehouses. However, this was 30–40 years ago and things have evolved quite a bit since then, and over time people started to see the problems that these data warehouse had, in particular it was hard to scale them, so this was good for the pristine small data that they had, but if they wanted to have lots of lots of data and they want to take it all in there it was hard to scale it, it was not elastic so they couldn’t just say okay We want to double the size of our data warehouse, that was a complicated operation and then it required this ETL to get your data into the data warehouse, that ETL process was very cumbersome so they did it once a week, so hence the data that they had in their data warehouse was stale, and they were actually operating on old data, so it wasn’t real time. Also, the companies have another important topic in mind, and that is AI. Artificial Intelligence  is one of the trending topics since many years ago, but the companies had all their data inside of data warehouse but they couldn’t ask to the data warehouse  to do prediction for them, because it just support SQL, and then finally it was all based on closed formats so once you had your data in this system it was hard to kind of get it out so you were locked into these data warehouse. Not future proof — Missing Predictions, Real-time, Scale So what happened is about 10 years ago the Hadoop vendor showed up and they said what you need is a Hadoop Data Lake, and what Hadoop Data Lake you will let you do is that you can now ETL all your data, it’s scalable thing so take all of your data and just dump it in to this open Data Lake and you can do all kinds of uses cases on top of it since these are open formats you can get all the machine learning, all this stuff working so all the problems that you had with your data warehouse will go away and you get all these benefits now with the Data Lake. So now you got massive scale so you could store petabytes of data in it, it was inexpensive, it was based on this open-source Hadoop technology, and it was based on open formats like Parquet and ORC, so you could easily get your data and move it around and it had the promise of AI  and real-time streaming, so that was really great people got really excited and started storing all their data in these data lakes in over the last 10 years they’ve been storing more and more of this. Become a cheap messy data store with poor performance A data lake  is a storage repository that inexpensively stores a vast amount of raw data, both current and historical, in native formats such as XML, JSON, CSV, and Parquet. It may contain operational relational databases with live transactional data. To extract meaningful information from a data lake, you must solve problems such as: Schema enforcement when new tables are introduced. Table repairs when any new data is inserted into the data lake. Frequent refreshes of metadata. Bottlenecks of small file sizes for distributed computations. Difficulty sorting data by an index if data is spread across many files and partitioned. AI applications are running into a lot of trouble, so the problem now that you have with the data lakes it’s kind of the same things that were the advantages that we had with the data warehouse, so we kind of lost them so in particular since you just dumped all your data there you lost thus pristine data with the control and the schemas on it and this data now is inconsistent it might be dirty it might not be throughout the petabyte of data set you can’t just rely that it’s just going to work, so it’s hard to build analytics on top of this data, and finally the performance actually is really bad because you’ve just dumped the data in there it hasn’t been optimized for your particular use case so a lot of companies are seeing that once they start building their applications on top of the data lake they don’t get the performance that they were getting from data warehouses earlier so, in short, you’ve got a messy data store with really poor performance. The hardest part of Artificial Intelligence is not Artificial Intelligence, in other words, Big Data was the Missing Link for AI, and most companies are struggling with Big Data. The current state of platforms So, what the enterprises are doing today? Which one of these has chosen? Data Lake or Data Warehouse? Enterprises with Data Lake and Data Warehouse The enterprises that they are using both, have also different problems: They just can have around 2 weeks of data into the data warehouse. The data warehouse is very expensive to scale. The architecture has poor agility in responding to new threats. The architecture has scale limitations, no historical data. The architecture needs more time and people to build than an architecture based on Delta Lake. What are some of the pain points of existing data pipelines? Introduction of new tables requires schema creation. Whenever any new data is inserted into the   data lake, table repairs are required. Metadata must be frequently refreshed. Small file sizes become a bottleneck for distributed computations. If data is sorted by a particular index (i.e. eventTime), it is very difficult to re-sort the data by a different index (i.e. userID) Delta Lake Delta Lake is an open source project that brings ACID transactions to Apache Spark™ and big data workloads. Key Features: ACID Transactions: Data lakes typically have multiple data pipelines reading and writing data concurrently, and data engineers have to go through a tedious process to ensure data integrity, due to the lack of transactions. Delta Lake brings ACID transactions to your data lakes. It provides serializability, the strongest level of isolation level. Scalable Metadata Handling: In big data, even the metadata itself can be “big data”. Delta Lake treats metadata just like data, leveraging Spark’s distributed processing power to handle all its metadata. As a result, Delta Lake can handle petabyte-scale tables with billions of partitions and files at ease. Time Travel (data versioning): Delta Lake provides snapshots of data enabling developers to access and revert to earlier versions of data for audits, rollbacks or to reproduce experiments. Open Format: All data in Delta Lake is stored in Apache Parquet format enabling Delta Lake to leverage the efficient compression and encoding schemes that are native to Parquet. Delta Lake uses versioned Parquet files to store your data in your cloud storage. Apart from the versions, Delta Lake also stores a transaction log to keep track of all the commits made to the table or blob store directory to provide ACID transactions. Unified Batch and Streaming Source and Sink: A table in Delta Lake is both a batch table, as well as a streaming source and sink. Streaming data ingest, batch historic backfill, and interactive queries all just work out of the box. Schema Enforcement: Delta Lake provides the ability to specify your schema and enforce it. This helps ensure that the data types are correct and required columns are present, preventing bad data from causing data corruption. Schema Evolution: Big data is continuously changing. Delta Lake enables you to make changes to a table schema that can be applied automatically, without the need for cumbersome DDL. 100% Compatible with Apache Spark API: Developers can use Delta Lake with their existing data pipelines with minimal change as it is fully compatible with Spark, the commonly used big data processing engine. DELETES/UPDATES/UPSERTS — Writers can modify a data set without interfering with jobs reading the data set. Automatic file management — Data access speeds up by organizing data into large files that can be read efficiently. Statistics and data skipping — Reads are 10–100x faster when statistics are tracked about the data in each file, allowing Delta to avoid reading irrelevant information. To avoid Data Swamp, or Data Cesspool. To solve problems related to schema on read. It’s the first unified data management system that unifies data warehousing with data lakes, in particular, it gives you the scale of the data lake, it gives you the reliability and performance of the data warehouse, and it gives you the low latency that you get out of real-time streaming engines. Delta Lake gets the good of the data lakes which is it gets that massive scale you store your data just on blob stores like Amazon S3, Azure Data Lake, HDFS, etc. Enables predictions, real-time and ad-hoc analytics at Massive Scale It’s based on open formats so Parquet and ORC you can just have your data in those formats that you can read elsewhere but you can also do predictions, machine learning and real-time streaming with it but in addition to that Delta Lake stores control delta and makes sure that your data is reliable so you get the good of the data warehouse as well so we try to keep your data really pristine in Delta Lake we give your transactional reliability so that you don’t get failures you just have atomic transactions and you get really fast queries there’s a lot of optimizations that go in this data when you move it into the system we see anywhere between 10 to 100 X speed-up over just if you took just Apache Spark and ran it on a traditional data lake yourself, so in short it enables predictions real-time and ad-hoc analytics at massive scale. Delta Lake  is very important for a data lake that incorporates streaming data because refreshing frequent metadata, repairs table, and it is able to accumulate small files on a secondly or minutely basis. Delta Lake Under the Hood The massive scale comes from the fact that we decouple compute in storage so it’s not coupled appliance like traditional data warehouse instead your data is stored on a blob store like Amazon S3, and you don’t have to pay a lot for that, these blob stores are quite cheap today independently you can scale your compute as you need so as more and more people use the system during the day it automatically scales up and down so you get this elasticity so that’s how we get massive scale. Reliability, that’s through asset transactions and data validation we actually validate your data when it comes in so that it actually follows the schema and if doesn’t we actually reject it and give you a chance to fix that. The performance we’ve added the traditional data warehouse techniques into Delta Lake so that we do indexing and caching of your data that way we can get the 10-100x speed The whole system is based on Structured Streaming from Apache Spark so you get the real-time streaming to ingest that spark provides to you. Delta Architecture The Delta Architecture is an evolution of Kappa Architecture and solves the problems that Lambda Architecture has. Example of Lambda Architecture Lambda Architecture has problems like: Historical queries: We have to build two separate pipelines, which can be very complex, and, ultimately, difficult to combine the processing of batch and real-time data, in other words, you need to duplicate your pipeline, one for streaming, and another for batching. Messy data: it turns out when different teams putting events into a Stream Processing System like Kafka Stream, Kinesis or Event Hub, it is important to have validation process, to check both the streaming version and the batch version. Mistakes and failures: that validation is great but it’s actually too late by the time it’s gone off you know that bad data has already been produced it’s in the data lake and so we need some way to correct it and this is actually pretty difficult especially in a distributed system, it’s not only human errors, also the spot price is spiked and my cluster died and half of the results were written out and now we have to clean it up, so a pretty typical technique here is if you have a partition by some granularity, let’s say by date or by hour and so now I have these nice clean boundaries, and we can build a bunch of processes that allows doing reprocessing, so if anything goes wrong We can fix the mistake and then just go back and reprocess. Query Performance: Millions of files are keeping on S3 without compaction, so the result is that a query on this data may be very slow due to network latency and volume of file metadata. The Delta Architecture is a vast improvement upon the traditional   Lambda Architecture. In the case of Delta Lake, we can do queries with performance similar to data warehouse because Delta Lake maintains logs and indexes, that allows us to skip and read only the data that’s necessary. With the previous architecture we had to manually handle unifying streaming and batch We had to do manual validation We had to worry about the logic and kind of the boundaries at which We was doing reprocessing We also had to handle compaction, with Delta Lake axes this unified store where I can take for a data from a variety of sources whether it’s Kafka, Kinesis or whatever We can put there and then all of my users can read it kind of with the strong guarantees of a data warehouse but really the nice thing here is we still maintain the scale of a data lake we have transaction we get reliability, we have indexes, we get performance and it integrates deeply with Spark so we can do latency streaming. Delta Lake delivers a powerful transactional storage layer by harnessing the power of Apache Spark and Databricks File System (DBFS). It is a single data management tool that combines the scale of a data lake, the reliability, and performance of a data warehouse, and the low latency of streaming in a single system. The core abstraction of Delta Lake is an optimized Spark table that stores data as parquet files in DBFS and maintains a transaction log that tracks changes to the table. Bronze level or row tables: Text files, RDBMS data, and streaming data are collected into the bronze level. Raw tables capture streaming and historical data into a permanent record (streaming data tends to disappear after a short while). Though, it’s generally hard to query. Silver level or query tables: A raw table is then parsed into query tables, also known as silver. Query tables consist of normalized raw data that is easier to query. Gold level or summary tables: Summary tables, also known as gold tables, are business-level aggregates. They’re often used for reporting, dashboards, and aggregations such as daily active website users. Summary tables contain aggregated key business metrics that are queried frequently, but the silver queries themselves would take too long. Platinum level or visualization level: It is the layer for creating business reports, dashboards, and visualizations. Diamond or dAImond level: It is the layer that contains Artificial Intelligence logic, can obtain the data from the bronze, silver, or gold level. In that layer solutions like Mlflow, Koalas, TensorFrames, and others will be running. This unified approach means that there is less complexity due to the removal of storage systems and data management steps, and, more importantly, output queries can be performed on streaming and historical data at the same time. The difference between Lambda and Delta architecture is that with Delta architecture, output queries can be performed on streaming and historical data at the same time. In Lambda architecture, streaming and historical data are treated as two separate branches feeding output queries. Enthusiastic computer scientist dedicated to the exploration and advancement of data and artificial intelligence technologies.

Source: 2026-05-31 Day04/10: Apache Iceberg Architecture Explained: Metadata Trees, Snapshots, and Partition Evolution | by Jatan Sahu | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Day04/10: Apache Iceberg Architecture Explained: Metadata Trees, Snapshots, and Partition Evolution | by Jatan Sahu | Medium url: https://medium.com/@Jatansahu/day04-10-apache-iceberg-architecture-explained-metadata-trees-snapshots-and-partition-evolution-8ffda7ff7a39 date: 2026-05-31T00:46:15.523Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Introduction As data lakes grew larger, a major problem appeared. Object storage systems like S3 or ADLS are not optimized for listing millions of files. Many early data lake engines relied on scanning directories to find data files. This becomes extremely slow when tables contain millions of files. Apache Iceberg was designed to solve this problem. Instead of relying on file listings, Iceberg introduces a metadata tree architecture that tracks every file in the dataset. The Core Idea Behind Iceberg Iceberg separates data storage from metadata management. Data is still stored as Parquet, ORC, or Avro files. But Iceberg manages those files through metadata layers. This allows: atomic commits snapshot isolation scalable metadata management Iceberg Table Architecture Iceberg organizes metadata in multiple layers. Metadata File The metadata file acts as the entry point for the table. It contains: schema partition specification snapshot references table properties Every commit updates the metadata file. Manifest List The manifest list tracks groups of manifest files. Each snapshot references a manifest list. Manifest Files Manifest files contain references to actual data files. They store metadata like: file path partition values record counts statistics This allows query engines to skip unnecessary files. Snapshot Architecture Each table write creates a snapshot. Snapshots represent consistent versions of the dataset. Example: Queries always read a specific snapshot. Hidden Partitioning One of Iceberg’s most powerful features is hidden partitioning. In traditional Hive tables, users must specify partitions manually. Example: If the table is partitioned by order_date, the query works efficiently. But if the query uses a different filter, partition pruning may fail. Iceberg solves this by automatically managing partition logic internally. Users do not need to know how data is partitioned. Schema Evolution Iceberg allows safe schema changes. Supported operations include: add column rename column reorder columns drop columns This avoids issues common in Hive tables. Architecture Diagram Advantages of Iceberg Iceberg was designed to solve major data lake limitations. Key benefits include: scalable metadata management reliable schema evolution snapshot isolation hidden partitioning Interview Questions What problem does Iceberg solve in data lakes? Iceberg solves metadata scalability issues and provides ACID transactions on large datasets. What is a manifest file? A manifest file contains metadata about data files, including file paths, partition values, and statistics. What is hidden partitioning? Hidden partitioning allows Iceberg to manage partitions internally without exposing them to users. What is a snapshot in Iceberg? A snapshot represents a consistent version of a dataset at a specific time. How does Iceberg avoid expensive file listings? By maintaining metadata structures that track all data files. Difference between Iceberg and Hive tables Hive relies on directory listings, while Iceberg manages file metadata through manifests. Conclusion Iceberg introduced a scalable metadata architecture for large data lakes. Instead of relying on filesystem operations, it manages datasets using structured metadata layers. In the next article we will explore Delta Lake and its transaction log architecture.

Source: A 2026 Introduction to Apache Iceberg - DEV Community
URL: https://dev.to/alexmercedcoder/a-2026-introduction-to-apache-iceberg-4nd8
A 2026 Introduction to Apache Iceberg - DEV Community Skip to content Powered by Algolia Log in Create account DEV Community 0 Add reaction 0 Like  0 Unicorn  0 Exploding Head  0 Raised Hands  0 Fire 0 Jump to Comments 1 Save Boost Copy link Copied to Clipboard Share to X Share to LinkedIn Share to Facebook Share to Mastodon Report Abuse Alex Merced Posted on Feb 13 A 2026 Introduction to Apache Iceberg
beginners # dataengineering # opensource # tutorial
Apache Iceberg is an open-source table format for large analytic datasets. It defines how data files stored on object storage (S3, ADLS, GCS) are organized into a logical table with a schema, partition layout, and consistent point-in-time snapshots. If you've heard the term "data lakehouse," Iceberg is the layer that makes it possible by bringing warehouse-grade reliability to data lake storage. This post covers what Iceberg is, how its metadata works under the hood, what changed across specification versions 1 through 3, what's being proposed for v4, and how to get started using Iceberg tables with Dremio in about ten minutes. Where Iceberg Came From Before Iceberg, most data lake tables used the Hive table format. Hive tracks data by directory paths: one directory per partition, with files inside. That works fine for small tables, but it breaks down at scale. Listing files across thousands of partition directories takes minutes. Schema changes require careful coordination. There's no isolation between readers and writers, so concurrent queries can return inconsistent results. Netflix hit all of these problems in production around 2017. Ryan Blue and Dan Weeks designed Iceberg to solve them by tracking individual files instead of directories, using file-level metadata instead of a central metastore, and requiring atomic commits for every change. Netflix open-sourced the project, and it entered the Apache Incubator in 2018. By May 2020, Iceberg graduated to an Apache Top-Level Project. Today it's the de facto open table format, adopted by AWS, Google, Snowflake, Databricks, Dremio, Cloudera, and dozens of other vendors. How Iceberg's Metadata Works Iceberg replaces directory listings with a tree of metadata files. Each layer in the tree stores progressively finer details about the table's contents. Catalog Pointer: The catalog (Polaris, Glue, Nessie, or any REST catalog implementation) stores a single pointer to the current metadata file. This is the entry point. Metadata File (JSON): Contains the current schema, partition specs, sort orders, snapshot list, and table properties. Every write creates a new metadata file and atomically swaps the catalog pointer. Manifest List (Avro): One per snapshot. Lists all manifest files belonging to that snapshot, along with partition-level summary stats. Query engines use these stats to skip entire manifests that can't match a query's filter. Manifest Files (Avro): Each manifest tracks a set of data files and stores per-file statistics: file path, partition tuple, record count, and column-level min, max, and null counts. These stats enable file-level pruning during scan planning. Data Files (Parquet/ORC/Avro): The actual rows, stored in columnar format. Iceberg itself is format-agnostic, though Parquet is the most common choice. This structure means scan planning is O(1) in metadata lookups rather than O(n) in partition directories. That's the core architectural advantage. Spec Versions: V1 Through V3 (and V4 Proposals) Version 1: Analytic Tables (2017–2020) V1 established the fundamentals: immutable data files, snapshot-based tracking, manifest-level file stats, hidden partitioning, and schema evolution via unique column IDs. Operations were limited to appends and full-partition overwrites. Version 2: Row-Level Deletes (~2022) V2 added delete files that encode which rows to remove from existing data files. Position delete files list specific (file, row-number) pairs. Equality delete files specify column values that identify deleted rows. This made UPDATE, DELETE, and MERGE possible without rewriting entire data files. V2 also introduced sequence numbers for ordering concurrent writes and resolving commit conflicts through optimistic concurrency. Version 3: Extended Capabilities (May 2025) V3 brought several major additions: Deletion Vectors: Binary bitmaps stored in Puffin files that replace position deletes. More compact in storage and faster to apply during reads. At most one deletion vector per data file. Row Lineage: Per-snapshot tracking of row-level identity ( first-row-id , added-rows ). This enables efficient change data capture (CDC) pipelines directly on Iceberg tables. New Data Types: variant for semi-structured data, geometry and geography for geospatial workloads, and nanosecond-precision timestamps ( timestamp_ns , timestamptz_ns ). Default Values: Columns can specify write-default and initial-default values, making schema evolution smoother. Multi-Argument Transforms: Partition and sort transforms can accept multiple input columns. Table Encryption Keys: Built-in support for encrypting data at rest. Version 4: Active Proposals (2025–2026) The community is actively discussing several changes for a future v4 spec: Single-file commits would consolidate all metadata changes into one file per commit, reducing I/O overhead for high-write workloads. Parquet for metadata would replace Avro-encoded metadata files with Parquet, enabling columnar reads of metadata (only load the fields you need). Relative path support would store file references relative to the table root, simplifying table migration and replication without metadata rewrites. Improved column statistics would add more granular stats for better query planning and change detection. Key Features Worth Knowing ACID Transactions: Every commit is atomic with serializable isolation. Readers never see partial writes. Schema Evolution: Add, drop, rename, or reorder columns safely. Iceberg uses unique field IDs, so renaming a column doesn't break older data files. Partition Evolution: Change your partitioning strategy without rewriting existing data. Old and new partition layouts coexist. Queries filter on data values, not partition columns. Hidden Partitioning: Users query raw values ( WHERE order_date = '2025-06-15' ). Iceberg applies transforms ( month , day , bucket , truncate ) automatically. No synthetic partition columns in the schema. Time Travel: Query any previous snapshot by ID or timestamp. Roll back to a known-good state in one command. Branching and Tagging: Named references to specific snapshots, useful for write-audit-publish workflows and staging environments. Multi-Engine Access: The same Iceberg table is readable and writable from Spark, Flink, Trino, Dremio, DuckDB, Snowflake, BigQuery, Presto, and others. The Value of the REST Catalog Spec Iceberg's REST Catalog Specification defines an HTTP API for table management. Any engine that speaks HTTP can create, list, read, and commit to Iceberg tables without importing a Java SDK. That's significant because it makes catalog access language-agnostic (Python, Rust, Go, JavaScript) and cloud-agnostic (AWS, GCP, Azure). It also enables server-side features like credential vending (short-lived storage tokens per request), commit deconfliction, and multi-table transactions. Several projects implement the REST Catalog spec: Apache Polaris, Project Nessie, Unity Catalog, AWS Glue (via adapter), and Snowflake Open Catalog. This means you can pick a catalog implementation without locking in your query engines. Every engine points at the same REST endpoint. Getting Started: Apache Iceberg on Dremio You can get hands-on with Iceberg tables right now using Dremio Cloud. Here's the quick path:
Sign up at dremio.com/get-started. You'll get a free 30-day trial. Dremio creates a lakehouse project and an Open Catalog (powered by Apache Polaris) automatically at signup.
Create an Iceberg table and insert data: Notice the PARTITION BY (MONTH(order_date)) . That's hidden partitioning in action. You query order_date directly; Iceberg handles the partitioning.
Query the metadata tables. Dremio exposes Iceberg's metadata through TABLE() functions. These let you inspect the internal state of your table without touching the raw metadata files: The table_snapshot query shows each snapshot ID, timestamp, and the operation that created it (append, overwrite, delete). The table_manifests query reveals how many data files and delete files exist in each manifest. Run these after each INSERT or DELETE to see how Iceberg tracks changes internally. Go Deeper This post covers the essentials, but Iceberg's spec and ecosystem run deep. If you want the full picture, three books cover the subject end to end: Apache Iceberg: The Definitive Guide (O'Reilly) by Tomer Shiran, Jason Hughes, and Alex Merced. Free download from Dremio. Apache Polaris: The Definitive Guide (O'Reilly) by Alex Merced, Andrew Madson, and Tomer Shiran. Free download from Dremio. Architecting an Apache Iceberg Lakehouse (Manning) by Alex Merced. A hands-on guide to designing modular lakehouse architectures with Spark, Flink, Dremio, and Polaris. Between these three resources and a free Dremio Cloud trial, you'll have everything you need to build on Apache Iceberg in production. Source: A Deep Dive into Apache Parquet with ClickHouse - Part 2
